During the Age of the Enlightenment any residual interest in Mary within Protestant churches almost disappeared, although Anglicans and Lutherans continued to honor her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-protestantencyclopedia_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-protestantencyclopedia-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Protestants acknowledge that Mary is "blessed among women"<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+1%3A42&amp;version=9" rel="nofollow">[Luke 1:42]</a></sup> but they do not agree that Mary is to be venerated, as worship of any person or thing other than God could be construed as idolatry (Exodus 20:3). She is considered to be an outstanding example of a life dedicated to God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 20th century, Protestants reacted in opposition to the Catholic dogma of the <a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_Mary" title="Assumption of Mary">Assumption of Mary</a>. The conservative tone of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">Second Vatican Council</a> began to mend the ecumenical differences, and Protestants began to show interest in Marian themes. In 1997 and 1998 ecumenical dialogs between Catholics and Protestants took place, but to date the majority of Protestants pay scant attention to Marian issues and often view them as a challenge to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sola_Scriptura" title="Sola Scriptura">authority of Scripture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-protestantencyclopedia_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-protestantencyclopedia-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglican">Anglican</span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Marian_theology" title="Anglican Marian theology">Anglican Marian theology</a></div>
<p>The multiple churches that form the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continuing_Anglican" title="Continuing Anglican">Continuing Anglican</a> movement have different views on Marian doctrines and venerative practices given that there is no single church with universal authority within the Communion and that the mother church (the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>) understands itself to be both "catholic" and "<a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformed</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Thus unlike the Protestant churches at large, the Anglican Communion (which includes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_USA" title="Episcopal Church USA">Episcopal Church</a> in the United States) includes segments which still retain some veneration of Mary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroedel81_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schroedel81-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mary's special position within God's purpose of salvation as "God-bearer" <i>(<a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a>)</i> is recognised in a number of ways by some Anglican Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braaten_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Braaten-112">[112]</a></sup> All the member churches of the Anglican Communion affirm in the historic creeds that Jesus was born of the Virgin Mary, and celebrates the feast days of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presentation_of_Christ_in_the_Temple" title="Presentation of Christ in the Temple">Presentation of Christ in the Temple</a>. This feast is called in older <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">prayer books</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purification_of_the_Blessed_Virgin_Mary" title="Purification of the Blessed Virgin Mary">Purification of the Blessed Virgin Mary</a> on February 2. The <a href="/wiki/Annunciation" title="Annunciation">Annunciation</a> of our Lord to the Blessed Virgin on March 25 was from before the time of <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> until the 18th century New Year's Day in England. The Annunciation is called the "Annunciation of our Lady" in the 1662 <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a>. Anglicans also celebrate in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visitation_(Christian)" title="Visitation (Christian)">Visitation of the Blessed Virgin</a> on 31 May, though in some provinces the traditional date of July 2 is kept. The feast of the St. Mary the Virgin is observed on the traditional day of the Assumption, August 15. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nativity_of_the_Blessed_Virgin_Mary" title="Nativity of the Blessed Virgin Mary">Nativity</a> of the Blessed Virgin is kept on September 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroedel81_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schroedel81-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Conception of the Blessed Virgin Mary is kept in the 1662 Book of Common Prayer, on December 8. In certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Catholic" title="Anglo-Catholic">Anglo-Catholic</a> parishes this feast is called the <a href="/wiki/Immaculate_Conception" title="Immaculate Conception">Immaculate Conception</a>. Again, the <a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_Mary" title="Assumption of Mary">Assumption of Mary</a> is believed in by most Anglo-Catholics, but is considered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pious" title="Pious">pious</a> opinion by moderate Anglicans. Protestant minded Anglicans reject the celebration of these feasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroedel81_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-Schroedel81-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prayers and venerative practices vary a great deal. For instance, as of the 19th century, following the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Movement" title="Oxford Movement">Oxford Movement</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Catholic" title="Anglo-Catholic">Anglo-Catholics</a> frequently pray the <a href="/wiki/Rosary" title="Rosary">Rosary</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Angelus" title="Angelus">Angelus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regina_Caeli" title="Regina Caeli">Regina Caeli</a>, and other litanies and anthems of Our Lady that are reminiscent of Catholic practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Low_church" title="Low church">Low-church</a> Anglicans rarely invoke the Blessed Virgin except in certain hymns, such as the second stanza of <a href="/wiki/Ye_Watchers_and_Ye_Holy_Ones" title="Ye Watchers and Ye Holy Ones">Ye Watchers and Ye Holy Ones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braaten_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Braaten-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Mary_(Anglican)" title="Society of Mary (Anglican)">Anglican Society of Mary</a> was formed in 1931 and maintains chapters in many countries. The purpose of the society is to foster devotion to Mary among Anglicans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroedel81_70-4"><a href="#cite_note-Schroedel81-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/High_church" title="High church">high-church</a> Anglicans espouse doctrines that are closer to Roman Catholics, and retain veneration for Mary, e.g., official Anglican pilgrimages to <a href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Lourdes" title="Our Lady of Lourdes">Our Lady of Lourdes</a> have taken place since 1963, and pilgrimages to <a href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Walsingham" title="Our Lady of Walsingham">Our Lady of Walsingham</a> have gone on for hundreds of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically, there has been enough common ground between Roman Catholics and Anglicans on Marian issues that in 2005 a joint statement called <i>Mary: grace and hope in Christ</i> was produced through ecumenical meetings of Anglicans and Roman Catholic theologians. This document, informally known as the "Seattle Statement", is not formally endorsed by either the Catholic Church or the Anglican Communion, but is viewed by its authors as the beginning of a joint understanding of Mary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroedel81_70-5"><a href="#cite_note-Schroedel81-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Lutheran">Lutheran</span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_Marian_theology" title="Luther's Marian theology">Luther's Marian theology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MaryattheTomb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4020" data-file-width="1744" height="346" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/MaryattheTomb.jpg/150px-MaryattheTomb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/MaryattheTomb.jpg/225px-MaryattheTomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/MaryattheTomb.jpg/300px-MaryattheTomb.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MaryattheTomb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stained glass of <a href="/wiki/Christ_taking_leave_of_his_Mother" title="Christ taking leave of his Mother">Jesus leaving his mother</a> in a <a href="/wiki/St._Matthew%27s_German_Evangelical_Lutheran_Church" title="St. Matthew's German Evangelical Lutheran Church">Lutheran Church</a>, South Carolina.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>'s harsh polemics against his Roman Catholic opponents over issues concerning Mary and the saints, theologians appear to agree that Luther adhered to the Marian decrees of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_council" title="Ecumenical council">ecumenical councils</a> and dogmas of the church. He held fast to the belief that Mary was a perpetual virgin and Mother of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroedel_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schroedel-119">[119]</a></sup> Special attention is given to the assertion that Luther, some three-hundred years before the dogmatization of the <a href="/wiki/Immaculate_Conception" title="Immaculate Conception">Immaculate Conception</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_IX" title="Pope Pius IX">Pope Pius IX</a> in 1854, was a firm adherent of that view. Others maintain that Luther in later years changed his position on the Immaculate Conception, which, at that time was undefined in the Church, maintaining however the sinlessness of Mary throughout her life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> For Luther, early in his life, the <a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_Mary" title="Assumption of Mary">Assumption of Mary</a> was an understood fact, although he later stated that the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> did not say anything about it and stopped celebrating its feast. Important to him was the belief that Mary and the saints do live on after death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> "Throughout his career as a priest-professor-reformer, Luther preached, taught, and argued about the veneration of Mary with a verbosity that ranged from childlike piety to sophisticated polemics. His views are intimately linked to his Christocentric theology and its consequences for liturgy and piety."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Luther, while revering Mary, came to criticize the "Papists" for blurring the line, between high admiration of the grace of God wherever it is seen in a human being, and religious service given to another creature. He considered the Roman Catholic practice of celebrating <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>' days and making intercessory requests addressed especially to Mary and other departed saints to be <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolatry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> His final thoughts on Marian devotion and veneration are preserved in a sermon preached at Wittenberg only a month before his death:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Therefore, when we preach faith, that we should worship nothing but God alone, the Father of our Lord Jesus Christ, as we say in the Creed: 'I believe in God the Father almighty and in Jesus Christ,' then we are remaining in the temple at Jerusalem. Again,'This is my beloved Son; listen to him.' 'You will find him in a manger'. He alone does it. But reason says the opposite:<br/>
<br/>
What, us? Are we to worship only Christ? Indeed, shouldn’t we also honor the holy mother of Christ? She is the woman who bruised the head of the serpent. Hear us, Mary, for thy Son so honors thee that he can refuse thee nothing. Here <a href="/wiki/Bernard_of_Clairvaux" title="Bernard of Clairvaux">Bernard</a> went too far in his <i>Homilies on the Gospel: Missus est Angelus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> God has commanded that we should honor the parents; therefore I will call upon Mary. She will intercede for me with the Son, and the Son with the Father, who will listen to the Son. So you have the picture of God as angry and Christ as judge; Mary shows to Christ her breast and Christ shows his wounds to the wrathful Father. That’s the kind of thing this comely bride, the wisdom of reason cooks up: Mary is the mother of Christ, surely Christ will listen to her; Christ is a stern judge, therefore I will call upon St. George and St. Christopher. No, we have been by God’s command baptized in the name of the Father, the Son, and the Holy Spirit, just as the Jews were circumcised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Certain Lutheran churches such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Lutheran_Catholic_Church" title="Anglo-Lutheran Catholic Church">Anglo-Lutheran Catholic Church</a> however, continue to venerate Mary and the saints in the same manner that Roman Catholics do, and hold all Marian dogmas as part of their faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Methodist">Methodist</span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Saints_in_Methodism#Virgin_Mary" title="Saints in Methodism">Saints in Methodism § Virgin Mary</a></div>
<p><span id="Methodism"></span></p>
<p>Methodists do not have any additional teachings on the Virgin Mary except from what is mentioned in Scripture and the ecumenical Creeds. As such, Methodists accept the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">Virgin Birth</a>, but reject the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Immaculate_Conception" title="Immaculate Conception">Immaculate Conception</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a>, the principal founder of the Methodist movement within the Church of England, believed that Mary "continued a <a href="/wiki/Perpetual_virginity_of_Mary" title="Perpetual virginity of Mary">pure and unspotted virgin</a>", thus upholding the doctrine of the perpetual virginity of Mary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davidmacd.com_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-davidmacd.com-134">[134]</a></sup> Contemporary Methodism does hold that Mary was a virgin before, during, and immediately after the birth of Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> In addition, some Methodists also hold the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_Mary" title="Assumption of Mary">Assumption of Mary</a> as a pious opinion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nontrinitarian">Nontrinitarian</span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nontrinitarians" title="Nontrinitarians">Nontrinitarians</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christadelphians" title="Christadelphians">Christadelphians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saint_movement" title="Latter Day Saint movement">Latter Day Saints</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> also acknowledge Mary as the biological mother of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>, but reject any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immaculate_conception" title="Immaculate conception">immaculate conception</a> and do not recognize Marian titles such as "Mother of God" as these groups mostly reject Christ's divinity. The <a href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saint_views_on_Mary" title="Latter Day Saint views on Mary">Latter Day Saint movement's view</a> affirms the virgin birth of Jesus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> and Christ's divinity but only as a separate being than <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">God the Father</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Mormon" title="Book of Mormon">Book of Mormon</a>, refers to Mary by name in prophecies and describes her as "most beautiful and fair above all other virgins"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> and as a "precious and chosen vessel."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since most Nontrinitarian groups are typically also <a href="/wiki/Christian_mortalism" title="Christian mortalism">Christian mortalism</a>, Mary is not seen as an intercessor between humankind and Jesus, whom mortalists would consider "asleep", awaiting resurrection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EOM_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-EOM-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish">Jewish</span></h3>
<p>The issue of the parentage of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_the_Talmud" title="Jesus in the Talmud">Jesus in the Talmud</a> affects also the view of his mother. However, the Talmud does not mention Mary by name and is considerate rather than only polemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The story about Panthera is also found in the <a href="/wiki/Toledot_Yeshu" title="Toledot Yeshu">Toledot Yeshu</a>, the literary origins of which can not be traced with any certainty, and given that it is unlikely to go before the 4th century, the time is now far too late to include authentic remembrances of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> The Blackwell Companion to Jesus states that the Toledot Yeshu has no historical facts as such and was perhaps created as a tool for warding off conversions to Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> The name Panthera may be a distortion of the term <i>parthenos</i> (virgin) and <a href="/wiki/Raymond_E._Brown" title="Raymond E. Brown">Raymond E. Brown</a> considers the story of Panthera a fanciful explanation of the birth of Jesus that includes very little historical evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Van_Voorst" title="Robert Van Voorst">Robert Van Voorst</a> states that because Toledot Yeshu is a medieval document with its lack of a fixed form and orientation towards a popular audience, it is "most unlikely" to have reliable historical information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic">Islamic</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mary_in_Islam" title="Mary in Islam">Mary in Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:214px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Virgin_Mary_and_Jesus_(old_Persian_miniature).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="212" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f4/Virgin_Mary_and_Jesus_%28old_Persian_miniature%29.jpg" width="212"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Virgin_Mary_and_Jesus_(old_Persian_miniature).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">Persian miniature</a> of Mary and Jesus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Virgin Mary holds a singularly exalted place in <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and she is considered by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a> to have been the greatest woman in the history of humankind. The Islamic scripture recounts the Divine Promise given to Mary as being: "Mary! God has chosen thee, and purified thee; He hath chosen thee above all the women of creation" (3:42).</p>
<p>Moreover, Mary is the only woman named in the Qur'an and she is mentioned or referred to in the scripture a total of fifty times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Mary holds a singularly distinguished and honored position among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Female_figures_in_the_Qur%27an" title="Female figures in the Qur'an">women in the Qur'an</a>. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sura" title="Sura">Sura</a> (chapter) in the Qur'an is titled "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maryam_(sura)" title="Maryam (sura)">Maryam</a>" (Mary), which is the only Sura in the Qur'an named after a woman, in which the story of Mary (Maryam) and Jesus (Isa) is recounted according to the view of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Jesus in Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mary is often referred to by Muslims by the honorific title "sayedetina" (our lady). She is mentioned in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a> as the daughter of Imran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glasse_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glasse-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>She is the only woman directly named in the Qur'an; declared (uniquely along with Jesus) to be a "Sign of God" to humanity;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> as one who "guarded her chastity";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C66.7C12.7Cs.3Dns_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C66.7C12.7Cs.3Dns-154">[154]</a></sup> an "obedient one";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C66.7C12.7Cs.3Dns_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C66.7C12.7Cs.3Dns-154">[154]</a></sup> "chosen of her mother" and dedicated to Allah whilst still in the womb;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> uniquely (amongst women) "Accepted into service by God";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C3.7C37.7Cs.3Dns_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C3.7C37.7Cs.3Dns-156">[156]</a></sup> cared for by (one of the prophets as per Islam) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_Zechariah" title="Islamic view of Zechariah">Zakariya</a> (Zacharias);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C3.7C37.7Cs.3Dns_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C3.7C37.7Cs.3Dns-156">[156]</a></sup> that in her childhood she resided in the Temple and uniquely had access to Al-<a href="/wiki/Mihrab" title="Mihrab">Mihrab</a> (understood to be the <a href="/wiki/Holy_of_Holies" title="Holy of Holies">Holy of Holies</a>), and was provided with heavenly "provisions" by God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C3.7C37.7Cs.3Dns_156-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C3.7C37.7Cs.3Dns-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glasse_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glasse-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mary is also called a "Chosen One";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C3.7C42.7Cs.3Dns_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C3.7C42.7Cs.3Dns-157">[157]</a></sup> a "Purified One";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C3.7C42.7Cs.3Dns_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C3.7C42.7Cs.3Dns-157">[157]</a></sup> a "Truthful one";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> her child conceived through "a Word from God";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> and "exalted above all women of The Worlds/Universes (the material and heavenly worlds)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C3.7C42.7Cs.3Dns_157-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C3.7C42.7Cs.3Dns-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Qur'an relates detailed narrative accounts of Maryam (Mary) in two places, <span class="plainlinks">Qur'an <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/3:35" rel="nofollow">3:35–47</a> and <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/19:16" rel="nofollow">19:16–34</a></span>. These state beliefs in both the Immaculate Conception of Mary and the Virgin birth of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> The account given in Sura 19 is nearly identical with that in the Gospel according to <a href="/wiki/Luke_the_Evangelist" title="Luke the Evangelist">Luke</a>, and both of these (Luke, Sura 19) begin with an account of the visitation of an angel upon Zakariya (Zecharias) and "Good News of the birth of Yahya (John)", followed by the account of the annunciation. It mentions how Mary was informed by an angel that she would become the mother of Jesus through the actions of God alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Islamic tradition, Mary and Jesus were the only children who could not be touched by Satan at the moment of their birth, for God imposed a veil between them and Satan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> According to author <a href="/wiki/Shabbir_Akhtar" title="Shabbir Akhtar">Shabbir Akhtar</a>, the Islamic perspective on Mary's Immaculate Conception is compatible with the Catholic doctrine of the same topic. "O People of the Book! Do not go beyond the bounds in your religion, and do not say anything of Allah but the truth. The Messiah, Jesus son of Mary, was but a Messenger of God, and a Word of His (Power) which He conveyed to Mary, and a spirit from Him. So believe in Allah (as the One, Unique God), and His Messengers (including Jesus, as Messenger); and do not say: (Allah is one of) a trinity. Give up (this assertion) – (it is) for your own good (to do so). Allah is but One Allah ; All-Glorified He is in that He is absolutely above having a son. To Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and whatever is on the earth. And Allah suffices as the One to be relied on, to Whom affairs should be referred." Quran 4/171<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Qur'an says that Jesus was the result of a virgin birth. The most detailed account of the annunciation and birth of Jesus is provided in Suras 3 and 19 of the Qur'an, where it is written that God sent an angel to announce that she could shortly expect to bear a son, despite being a virgin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baha.27i">Baha'i</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá'í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a> venerates Mary as the mother of Jesus. The <i><a href="/wiki/Kit%C3%A1b-i-%C3%8Dq%C3%A1n" title="Kitáb-i-Íqán">Kitáb-i-Íqán</a></i>, the primary theological work of the Baha'i religion, describes Mary as "that most beauteous countenance," and "that veiled and immortal Countenance." It claims that Jesus was "conceived of the Holy Ghost."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Others">Others</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biblical_scholars">Biblical scholars</span></h4>
<p>The statement that Joseph "knew her not till she brought forth her first born son" (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matt.+1%3A25&amp;version=DRA" rel="nofollow">Matthew 1:25 DouayRheims</a></span>) has been debated among scholars, with some saying that she did not remain a virgin and some saying that she was a <a href="/wiki/Perpetual_virginity_of_Mary" title="Perpetual virginity of Mary">perpetual virgin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Other scholars contend that the Greek word <i>heos</i> (i.e., until) denotes a state up to a point, but does not mean that the state ended after that point, and that Matthew 1:25 does not confirm or deny the virginity of Mary after the birth of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> According to Biblical scholar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bart_Ehrman" title="Bart Ehrman">Bart Ehrman</a> the Hebrew word <i><a href="/wiki/Almah" title="Almah">almah</a></i>, meaning young woman of childbearing age, was translated into Greek as <i><a href="/wiki/Parthenos_(mythology)" title="Parthenos (mythology)">parthenos</a></i>, which only means virgin, in Isaiah 7:14, which is commonly believed by Christians to be the prophecy of the Virgin Mary referred to in Matthew 1:23.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> While Matthew and Luke give differing versions of the virgin birth, John quotes the uninitiated Philip and the disbelieving Jews gathered at Galilee referring to Joseph as Jesus's father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrman2008_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman2008-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrman1999_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman1999-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other biblical verses have also been debated, e.g., that the reference by Paul that Jesus was made "of the seed of David according to the flesh" (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Romans+1%3A3&amp;version=9" rel="nofollow">Romans 1:3</a></span>) may be interpreted as Joseph being the father of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> However, most scholars reject this interpretation in the context of <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">virgin birth</a> given that Paul used the Greek word <i>genomenos</i> (i.e., becoming) rather than the word <i>gennetos</i> (i.e., that is born, born)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> and the reference to "seed of David" is likely to Mary's lineage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Christian_Rome">Pre-Christian Rome</span></h4>
<p>From the early stages of Christianity, belief in the virginity of Mary and the virgin conception of Jesus, as stated in the gospels, holy and supernatural, was used by detractors, both political and religious, as a topic for discussions, debates and writings, specifically aimed to challenge the divinity of Jesus and thus Christians and Christianity alike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> In the 2nd century, as part of the earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Christian" title="Anti-Christian">anti-Christian</a> polemics, <a href="/wiki/Celsus" title="Celsus">Celsus</a> suggested that Jesus was the illegitimate son of a Roman soldier named Panthera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> The views of Celsus drew responses from <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a>, the Church Father in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Egypt" title="Alexandria, Egypt">Alexandria, Egypt</a>, who considered it a fabricated story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> How far Celsus sourced his view from Jewish sources remains a subject of discussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_devotion">Christian devotion</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marian_devotions" title="Marian devotions">Marian devotions</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gospa_Trsatska.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1508" data-file-width="2088" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Gospa_Trsatska.jpg/250px-Gospa_Trsatska.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Gospa_Trsatska.jpg/375px-Gospa_Trsatska.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Gospa_Trsatska.jpg/500px-Gospa_Trsatska.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gospa_Trsatska.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Icon with a Madonna and Child depiction, surrounded by key events in Mary's life. <a href="/wiki/Trsat" title="Trsat">Trsat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> (c. 12th century)</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2nd_to_5th_centuries">2nd to 5th centuries</span></h3>
<p>Christian devotion to Mary goes back to the 2nd century and predates the emergence of a specific Marian liturgical system in the 5th century, following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Ephesus" title="First Council of Ephesus">First Council of Ephesus</a> in 431. The Council itself was held at a church in Ephesus which had been dedicated to Mary about a hundred years before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> In Egypt the veneration of Mary had started in the 3rd century and the term <a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a> was used by <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Egypt" title="Alexandria, Egypt">Alexandrian</a> Father of the Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>The earliest known Marian prayer (the <i><a href="/wiki/Sub_tuum_praesidium" title="Sub tuum praesidium">Sub tuum praesidium</a></i>, or <i>Beneath Thy Protection</i>) is from the 3rd century (perhaps 270), and its text was rediscovered in 1917 on a papyrus in Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">Edict of Milan</a> in 313, by the 5th century artistic images of Mary began to appear in public and larger churches were being dedicated to Mary, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Basilica_di_Santa_Maria_Maggiore" title="Basilica di Santa Maria Maggiore">S. Maria Maggiore</a> in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="4th-century_Arabia">4th-century Arabia</span></h4>
<p>According to the 4th-century heresiologist <a href="/wiki/Epiphanius_of_Salamis" title="Epiphanius of Salamis">Epiphanius of Salamis</a> the Virgin Mary was worshipped as a <a href="/wiki/Mother_goddess" title="Mother goddess">mother goddess</a> in the Christian sect of <a href="/wiki/Collyridianism" title="Collyridianism">Collyridianism</a>, which was found throughout Arabia sometime during the 300s AD. Collyridianism had women performing priestly acts. They made bread offerings to the Virgin Mary. The group was condemned as heretical by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> and was preached against by <a href="/wiki/Epiphanius_of_Salamis" title="Epiphanius of Salamis">Epiphanius of Salamis</a>, who wrote about the group in his writings titled <a href="/wiki/Panarion" title="Panarion">Panarion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>The adoption of the mother of Jesus as a virtual goddess may represent a reintroduction of aspects of the worship of <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a>. According to Sabrina Higgins, "When looking at images of the Egyptian goddess Isis and those of the Virgin Mary, one may initially observe iconographic similarities. These parallels have led many scholars to suggest that there is a distinct iconographic relationship between Isis and Mary. In fact, some scholars have gone even further, and have suggested, on the basis of this relationship, a direct link between the cult of Mary and that of Isis."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> Conversely, Carl Olson and Sandra Miesel dispute the idea that Christianity copied elements of Isis's iconography, saying that the symbol of a mother and her child is part of the universal human experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DaVinciHoax_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-DaVinciHoax-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantium">Byzantium</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a> is a cultic centre of Mary, the site of the first Church dedicated to her and the rumoured place of her death. Ephesus was previously a centre for worship of <a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">Artemis</a> a virgin goddess. The <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Artemis" title="Temple of Artemis">Temple of Artemis</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a> being regarded as one of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_Ancient_World" title="Seven Wonders of the Ancient World">Seven Wonders of the Ancient World</a> The cult of Mary was furthered by Queen Theodora in the 6th Century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> According to William E. Phipps, in the book <i>Survivals of Roman Religion</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> "Gordon Laing argues convincingly that the worship of Artemis as both virgin and mother at the grand Ephesian temple contributed to the veneration of Mary."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span></h3>
<p>The Middle Ages saw many legends about Mary, and also her parents and even grandparents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Virgin's popularity increased dramatically starting in the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> This rise in popularity was linked to the Vatican's designation of Mary as the <a href="/wiki/Mediatrix" title="Mediatrix">mediatrix</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Depiction_in_Renaissance_art">Depiction in Renaissance art</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Angelico,_madonna_col_bambino,_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4722" data-file-width="3280" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg/170px-Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg/255px-Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg/340px-Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Angelico,_madonna_col_bambino,_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Madonna_of_humility" title="Madonna of humility">Madonna of humility</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Fra_Angelico" title="Fra Angelico">Fra Angelico</a>, c. 1430. A traditional depiction of Mary wearing blue clothes.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In paintings, Mary is traditionally portrayed in <a href="/wiki/Marian_blue" title="Marian blue">blue</a>. This tradition can trace its origin to the Byzantine Empire, from c.500 AD, where blue was "the colour of an empress". A more practical explanation for the use of this colour is that in Medieval and Renaissance Europe, the blue pigment was derived from the rock <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a>, a stone imported from Afghanistan of greater value than gold. Beyond a painter's retainer, patrons were expected to purchase any gold or lapis lazuli to be used in the painting. Hence, it was an expression of devotion and glorification to swathe the Virgin in gowns of blue. Transformations in visual depictions of the Virgin from the 13th to 15th centuries mirror her "social" standing within the Church as well as in society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_the_Reformation">Since the Reformation</span></h3>
<p>Over the centuries, devotion and veneration to Mary has varied greatly among Christian traditions. For instance, while Protestants show scant attention to Marian prayers or devotions, of all the saints whom the Orthodox venerate, the most honored is Mary, who is considered "more honorable than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cherubim" title="Cherubim">Cherubim</a> and more glorious than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seraphim" title="Seraphim">Seraphim</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fairbairn_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Fairbairn-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Orthodox theologian <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Bulgakov" title="Sergei Bulgakov">Sergei Bulgakov</a> wrote: "Love and veneration of the Blessed Virgin Mary is the soul of Orthodox piety. A faith in Christ which does not include his mother is another faith, another Christianity from that of the Orthodox church."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bulgakov_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bulgakov-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the Catholics and the Orthodox may honor and venerate Mary, they do not view her as divine, nor do they worship her. Roman Catholics view Mary as subordinate to Christ, but uniquely so, in that she is seen as above all other creatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> Similarly Theologian <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Bulgakov" title="Sergei Bulgakov">Sergei Bulgakov</a> wrote that the Orthodox view Mary as "superior to all created beings" and "ceaselessly pray for her intercession". However, she is not considered a "substitute for the One Mediator" who is Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bulgakov_100-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bulgakov-100">[100]</a></sup> "Let Mary be in honor, but let worship be given to the Lord", he wrote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> Similarly, Catholics do not worship Mary as a divine being, but rather "hyper-venerate" her. In Roman Catholic theology, the term <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperdulia" title="Hyperdulia">hyperdulia</a></i> is reserved for Marian veneration, <i><a href="/wiki/Latria" title="Latria">latria</a></i> for the worship of God, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dulia" title="Dulia">dulia</a></i> for the veneration of other saints and angels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> The definition of the three level hierarchy of <i>latria</i>, <i>hyperdulia</i> and <i>dulia</i> goes back to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Second Council of Nicaea</a> in 787.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>Devotions to artistic depictions of Mary vary among Christian traditions. There is a long tradition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Marian_art" title="Roman Catholic Marian art">Roman Catholic Marian art</a> and no image permeates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Art_in_Roman_Catholicism" title="Art in Roman Catholicism">Catholic art</a> as does the image of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madonna_and_Child" title="Madonna and Child">Madonna and Child</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> The icon of the Virgin Theotokos with Christ is without doubt the most venerated icon in the Orthodox Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> Both Roman Catholic and Orthodox Christians venerate images and icons of Mary, given that the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Second Council of Nicaea</a> in 787 permitted their veneration with the understanding that those who venerate the image are venerating the reality of the person it represents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> and the 842 Synod of Constantinople confirming the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> According to Orthodox piety and traditional practice, however, believers ought to pray before and venerate only flat, two-dimensional icons, and not three-dimensional statues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican</a> position towards Mary is in general more conciliatory than that of Protestants at large and in a book he wrote about praying with the icons of Mary, <a href="/wiki/Rowan_Williams" title="Rowan Williams">Rowan Williams</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, said: "It is not only that we cannot understand Mary without seeing her as pointing to Christ; we cannot understand Christ without seeing his attention to Mary."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroedel81_70-6"><a href="#cite_note-Schroedel81-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 4, 1781, 11 families of <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Pobladores" title="Los Angeles Pobladores"><i>pobladores</i></a> arrived from the Gulf of California and established a city in the name of <a href="/wiki/Charles_III_of_Spain" title="Charles III of Spain">King Carlos III</a>. The small town was named <i>El Pueblo de Nuestra Señora de los Ángeles de la Porciúncula</i> (after our Lady of the Angels), a city that today is known simply as <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marian_feasts">Marian feasts</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marian_feast_days" title="Marian feast days">Marian feast days</a></div>
<p>The earliest feasts that relate to Mary grew out of the cycle of feasts that celebrated the <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">Nativity of Jesus</a>. Given that according to the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Gospel of Luke</a> (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Luke#2:22" title="s:Bible (King James)/Luke">Luke 2:22-40</a>), forty days after the birth of Jesus, along with the <a href="/wiki/Presentation_of_Jesus_at_the_Temple" title="Presentation of Jesus at the Temple">Presentation of Jesus at the Temple</a> Mary was purified according to Jewish customs, the <i>Feast of the Purification</i> began to be celebrated by the 5th century, and became the "Feast of <a href="/wiki/Simeon_(Gospel_of_Luke)" title="Simeon (Gospel of Luke)">Simeon</a>" in <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaryClayton_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaryClayton-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pjazzasantamarijaghaxaqmalta.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="299" data-file-width="448" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Pjazzasantamarijaghaxaqmalta.JPG/220px-Pjazzasantamarijaghaxaqmalta.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Pjazzasantamarijaghaxaqmalta.JPG/330px-Pjazzasantamarijaghaxaqmalta.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Pjazzasantamarijaghaxaqmalta.JPG/440px-Pjazzasantamarijaghaxaqmalta.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pjazzasantamarijaghaxaqmalta.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Village decorations during the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Assumption" title="Feast of the Assumption">Feast of the Assumption</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/G%C4%A7axaq" title="Għaxaq">Għaxaq</a>, Malta</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 7th and 8th centuries four more Marian feasts were established in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">West</a>, a feast dedicated to Mary, just before Christmas was celebrated in the Churches of <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> in Italy in the 7th century. The four Roman Marian feasts of Purification, Annunciation, Assumption and Nativity of Mary were gradually and sporadically introduced into England by the 11th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaryClayton_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-MaryClayton-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over time, the number and nature of feasts (and the associated <a href="/wiki/Titles_of_Mary" title="Titles of Mary">Titles of Mary</a>) and the venerative practices that accompany them have varied a great deal among diverse Christian traditions. Overall, there are significantly more titles, feasts and venerative Marian practices among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholics" title="Roman Catholics">Roman Catholics</a> than any other Christians traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FrankFlinn_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-FrankFlinn-18">[18]</a></sup> Some such feasts relate to specific events, e.g., the Feast of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Victory" title="Our Lady of Victory">Our Lady of Victory</a> was based on the 1571 victory of the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lepanto_(1571)" title="Battle of Lepanto (1571)">Battle of Lepanto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
<p>Differences in feasts may also originate from doctrinal issues—the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Assumption" title="Feast of the Assumption">Feast of the Assumption</a> is such an example. Given that there is no agreement among all Christians on the circumstances of the death, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dormition_of_the_Theotokos" title="Dormition of the Theotokos">Dormition</a> or <a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_Mary" title="Assumption of Mary">Assumption of Mary</a>, the feast of assumption is celebrated among some denominations and not others. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillerbrand_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hillerbrand-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> While the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> celebrates the Feast of the Assumption on August 15, some <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">Eastern Catholics</a> celebrate it as <a href="/wiki/Dormition_of_the_Mother_of_God" title="Dormition of the Mother of God">Dormition of the Theotokos</a>, and may do so on August 28, if they follow the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> also celebrate it as the Dormition of the Theotokos, one of their 12 <a href="/wiki/Great_Feasts_of_the_Orthodox_Church" title="Great Feasts of the Orthodox Church">Great Feasts</a>. Protestants do not celebrate this, or any other Marian feasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillerbrand_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hillerbrand-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_Mariology">Catholic Mariology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gettorf_Altar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2487" data-file-width="2760" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Gettorf_Altar.jpg/220px-Gettorf_Altar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Gettorf_Altar.jpg/330px-Gettorf_Altar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Gettorf_Altar.jpg/440px-Gettorf_Altar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gettorf_Altar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mary with an inscription referencing Luke1:46-47 in St.Jürgen church in <a href="/wiki/Gettorf" title="Gettorf">Gettorf</a> (Schleswig-Holstein)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mariology" title="Mariology">Mariology</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Mariology" title="Roman Catholic Mariology">Roman Catholic Mariology</a></div>
<p>There is significant diversity in the Marian doctrines attributed to her primarily by the Roman Catholic Church. The key Marian doctrines held primarily in Catholicism can be briefly outlined as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Immaculate_Conception" title="Immaculate Conception">Immaculate Conception</a>: Mary was conceived without <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">original sin</a>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mother_of_God" title="Mother of God">Mother of God</a>: Mary, as the mother of Jesus, is the <a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a> (God-bearer), or Mother of God.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">Virgin birth of Jesus</a>: Mary conceived Jesus by action of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">Holy Spirit</a> while remaining a virgin.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perpetual_Virginity" title="Perpetual Virginity">Perpetual Virginity</a>: Mary remained a virgin all her life, even after the act of giving birth to Jesus.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dormition_of_the_Mother_of_God" title="Dormition of the Mother of God">Dormition</a>: commemorates Mary's "falling asleep" or natural death shortly before her Assumption.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_Mary" title="Assumption of Mary">Assumption</a>: Mary was taken <a href="/wiki/Entering_Heaven_alive" title="Entering Heaven alive">bodily into Heaven</a> either at, or before, her death.</li>
</ul>
<p>The acceptance of these Marian doctrines by Roman Catholics can be summarized as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-protestantencyclopedia_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-protestantencyclopedia-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th>Doctrine</th>
<th>Church action</th>
<th>Accepted by</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mother_of_God" title="Mother of God">Mother of God</a></td>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Ephesus" title="First Council of Ephesus">First Council of Ephesus</a>, 431</td>
<td>Roman Catholics, Eastern Orthodox, Oriental Orthodox, Anglicans, Lutherans, Methodists</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">Virgin birth of Jesus</a></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a>, 325</td>
<td>Roman Catholics, Eastern Orthodox, Oriental Orthodox, Assyrians, Anglicans, non-liberal Protestants</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_Mary" title="Assumption of Mary">Assumption of Mary</a></td>
<td><i><a href="/wiki/Munificentissimus_Deus" title="Munificentissimus Deus">Munificentissimus Deus</a></i> encyclical<br/>
<a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XII" title="Pope Pius XII">Pope Pius XII</a>, 1950</td>
<td>Roman Catholics, Eastern and Oriental Orthodox (only following her natural death), some Anglicans, some Lutherans</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Immaculate_Conception" title="Immaculate Conception">Immaculate Conception</a></td>
<td><i><a href="/wiki/Ineffabilis_Deus" title="Ineffabilis Deus">Ineffabilis Deus</a></i> encyclical<br/>
<a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_IX" title="Pope Pius IX">Pope Pius IX</a>, 1854</td>
<td>Roman Catholics, some Anglicans, some Lutherans (early Martin Luther)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perpetual_Virginity" title="Perpetual Virginity">Perpetual Virginity</a></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Second Council of Constantinople">Council of Constantinople</a>, 533<br/>
<a href="/wiki/Smalcald_Articles" title="Smalcald Articles">Smalcald Articles</a>, 1537</td>
<td>Roman Catholics, Eastern Orthodox, Oriental Orthodox, Assyrians, some Anglicans, some Lutherans (Martin Luther)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The title "Mother of God" (<a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a>) for Mary was confirmed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Ephesus" title="First Council of Ephesus">First Council of Ephesus</a>, held at the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Mary" title="Church of Mary">Church of Mary</a> in 431. The Council decreed that Mary is the Mother of God because her son Jesus is one person who is both God and man, divine and human.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccel.org_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-ccel.org-19">[19]</a></sup> This doctrine is widely accepted by Christians in general, and the term Mother of God had already been used within the oldest known prayer to Mary, the <a href="/wiki/Sub_tuum_praesidium" title="Sub tuum praesidium">Sub tuum praesidium</a> which dates to around 250 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">Virgin birth of Jesus</a> was an almost universally held belief among Christians from the 2nd until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-225">[225]</a></sup> It is included in the two most widely used <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creeds</a>, which state that Jesus "was incarnate of the Holy Spirit and the <i>Virgin</i> Mary" (the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a> in what is now its familiar form)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Creed" title="Apostles' Creed">Apostles' Creed</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel of Matthew</a> describes Mary as a virgin who fulfilled the prophecy of <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Isaiah+7%3A14&amp;version=9" rel="nofollow">Isaiah 7:14</a></span>, mistranslating the Hebrew word <i>alma</i> ("young woman") in Isaiah 7:14 as "virgin", though.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The authors of the Gospels of Matthew and <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Luke</a> consider Jesus' conception not the result of intercourse and assert that Mary had "no relations with man" before Jesus' birth.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mt+1%3A18&amp;version=9" rel="nofollow">[Mt 1:18]</a></sup> <sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mt+1%3A25&amp;version=9" rel="nofollow">[Mt 1:25]</a></sup> <sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Lk+1%3A34&amp;version=9" rel="nofollow">[Lk 1:34]</a></sup> This alludes to the belief that Mary conceived Jesus through the action of God the Holy Spirit, and not through <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">intercourse</a> with Joseph or anyone else.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miravalle56_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miravalle56-227">[227]</a></sup></p>
<p>The doctrines of the <a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_Mary" title="Assumption of Mary">Assumption</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dormition_of_the_Mother_of_God" title="Dormition of the Mother of God">Dormition</a> of Mary relate to her death and bodily assumption to <a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">Heaven</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> has dogmaically defined the doctrine of the Assumption, which was done in 1950 by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XII" title="Pope Pius XII">Pope Pius XII</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Munificentissimus_Deus" title="Munificentissimus Deus">Munificentissimus Deus</a></i>. Whether the Virgin Mary died or not is not defined dogmatically, however, although a reference to the death of Mary are made in <i>Munificentissimus Deus</i>. In the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>, the Assumption of the Virgin Mary is believed, and celebrated with her <a href="/wiki/Dormition_of_the_Mother_of_God" title="Dormition of the Mother of God">Dormition</a>, where they believe she died.</p>
<p>Roman Catholics believe in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immaculate_Conception_of_Mary" title="Immaculate Conception of Mary">Immaculate Conception of Mary</a>, as proclaimed <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ex_cathedra" title="Ex cathedra">ex cathedra</a></i> by Pope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pius_IX" title="Pius IX">Pius IX</a> in 1854, namely that she was filled with grace from the very moment of her conception in her mother's womb and preserved from the stain of original sin. The <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a> has a liturgical <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Immaculate_Conception" title="Feast of the Immaculate Conception">feast by that name</a>, kept on December 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> Orthodox Christians reject the Immaculate Conception dogma principally because their understanding of ancestral sin (the Greek term corresponding to the Latin "original sin") differs from the <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustinian</a> interpretation and that of the Catholic Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perpetual_Virginity" title="Perpetual Virginity">Perpetual Virginity</a> of Mary asserts Mary's real and perpetual <a href="/wiki/Virginity" title="Virginity">virginity</a> even in the act of giving birth to the Son of God made Man. The term Ever-Virgin (Greek <i><span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">ἀειπάρθενος</span></i>) is applied in this case, stating that Mary remained a virgin for the remainder of her life, making Jesus her biological and only son, whose <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">conception</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">birth</a> are held to be miraculous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fahlbusch_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fahlbusch-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miravalle56_227-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miravalle56-227">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> While the Orthodox Churches hold the position articulated in the <i><a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_James" title="Gospel of James">Protoevangelium of James</a></i> that Jesus' brothers and sisters are older children of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Joseph" title="Saint Joseph">Joseph the Betrothed</a>, step-siblings from an earlier marriage that left him widowed, Roman Catholic teaching follows the Latin father <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> in considering them Jesus' cousins.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinematic_portrayals">Cinematic portrayals</span></h2>
<p>Mary has been portrayed in various films and on television, including:</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Miracle_(1912_film)" title="The Miracle (1912 film)">The Miracle</a></i> (1912 color <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent film</a> of the play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Miracle_(play)" title="The Miracle (play)">The Miracle</a></i> (as a statue which comes to life))</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Das_Mirakel_(1912_film)" title="Das Mirakel (1912 film)">Das Mirakel</a></i> (1912) <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent film</a>; a German version of the play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Miracle_(play)" title="The Miracle (play)">The Miracle</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Song_of_Bernadette_(film)" title="The Song of Bernadette (film)">The Song of Bernadette</a></i> (1943 film)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Living_Christ_Series" title="The Living Christ Series">The Living Christ Series</a></i> (1951 non-theatrical, non-television film twelve-part series)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Miracle_of_Our_Lady_of_Fatima" title="The Miracle of Our Lady of Fatima">The Miracle of Our Lady of Fatima</a></i> (1952 film)</li>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Miracle_(1959_film)" title="The Miracle (1959 film)">The Miracle</a></i> (1959 film; a loose remake of the 1912 film <i><a href="/wiki/Das_Mirakel_(1912_film)" title="Das Mirakel (1912 film)">Das Mirakel</a></i>)</li>

<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Matthew#1:23" title="s:Bible (King James)/Matthew">Matthew 1:23</a> uses Greek <i>parthénos</i> virgin, whereas only the Hebrew of <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt1007.htm#14" rel="nofollow">Isaiah 7:14</a>, from which the New Testament ostensibly quotes, as <i>Almah</i> young maiden. See article on <i>parthénos</i> in Bauercc/(Arndt)/Gingrich/Danker, <i>A Greek-English Lexicon of the New Testament and Other Early Christian Literature</i>, Second Edition, University of Chicago Press, 1979, p. 627.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-137">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Stepp, Todd (December 23, 2009). "Theotokos; Mary, Mother of God". Wesleyan/Anglican Society. <q>We Protestants (for the most part) tend to say something to the affect [sic] that, if it is not found in Scripture it is not held to be required as an article of faith. Thus, the assumption of Mary would not be held as an article of faith (i.e., as a required doctrine). However, in as much as the Scripture does not say that Mary was not assumed into heaven, and, in as much as we do have other instances of some sort of "assumption" in Scripture (e.g., Elijah, as mentioned, before), there seems to be nothing that would require that a Protestant Christian could not have a private "opinion" (in the Wesleyan sense of the term) that agrees with Rome or Constantinople (at least regarding Mary's assumption).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMary%2C+mother+of+Jesus&amp;rft.aufirst=Todd&amp;rft.aulast=Stepp&amp;rft.btitle=Theotokos%3B+Mary%2C+Mother+of+God&amp;rft.date=2009-12-23&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=Wesleyan%2FAnglican+Society&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> <span class="error citation-comment" style="display:none;font-size:100%">Missing or empty <code style="color:inherit; border:inherit; padding:inherit;">|url=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#cite_web_url" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>);</span> <span class="error citation-comment" style="display:none;font-size:100%"><code style="color:inherit; border:inherit; padding:inherit;">|access-date=</code> requires <code style="color:inherit; border:inherit; padding:inherit;">|url=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#accessdate_missing_url" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>)</span></span></li>
